\begin{itemize}

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.

But she might be forced to choose a large number if she wants to win? Actually, she wants to win; she can choose numbers to try to make Bazza unable to move. So we need to analyze the game more systematically.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly the answer is: Alice wins for λ ≥ 1, Bazza wins for λ < 1? But we already saw that for λ=1, Alice wins. For λ=0.5, we need to check if Bazza can force a win. Let's try to see if Bazza can adopt a strategy to eventually make Alice unable to move. He can try to increase the sum of squares to the maximum allowed, and also try to make the sum as large as possible, so that at some odd n, the sum before Alice's move exceeds λ n. But note that Alice can always choose x_n = 0 to keep sum small, but if the sum before her turn is already large, she cannot choose 0? Actually, she can always choose 0; if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, then she can choose 0 and move. So the only way she loses is if S_{n-1} > λ n. So Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n. But S_{n-1} is the sum of x_i for i=1..n-1. Since Bazza controls even moves, he can try to increase the sum by choosing large x_n at even turns. However, he must keep Q_n ≤ n. So if he chooses large x_n, that increases Q_n, and he might be forced to keep Q_n ≤ n, so he cannot make x_n too large. There is a trade-off.

We can think of the game as a kind of "alternating constraints" where each player can choose numbers to maximize their own chance of winning. Perhaps we can find a "strategy stealing" or "potential function" that gives a threshold.

Let's define the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The game ends when a player cannot move. For Alice at odd n, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, he loses if Q_{n-1} > n.

Thus, the game is similar to a "take-away" where each player adds a nonnegative number, and the constraints are upper bounds on cumulative sums. The players can choose any number within the allowed range. They want to force the opponent into a position where the bound is exceeded.

We can try to analyze the game by considering the "critical" sequences. Perhaps we can find a strategy for Alice to win if λ ≥ 1, and for Bazza to win if λ < 1. But we need to verify for λ=1 we already found a winning strategy for Alice. For λ=0.5, we need to see if Bazza can force a win. Let's try to see if Bazza can adopt a strategy that eventually makes S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n. Since λ=0.5, λ n = n/2. So he wants to make the sum before Alice's turn exceed n/2. The sum is cumulative; at odd n, the sum before her turn is S_{n-1}. He can try to increase the sum by choosing large x at even turns. But he must also keep Q_n ≤ n. So if he chooses x_n large, that increases Q_n, and he might need to keep Q_n ≤ n. So there is a constraint linking the sum and sum of squares.

We can try to see if Bazza can force a win by playing a "greedy" strategy: always choose x_n as large as possible, i.e., such that Q_n = n (the maximum allowed). Then at even n, he sets x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Then the sum S_n increases by that amount. Then Alice at odd n+1 must have S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n is already > λ (n+1), she loses. So Bazza wants to make S_n large enough that at the next odd turn, S_n > λ (n+1). Since he controls even moves, he can try to push S_n up.

Let's simulate for λ=0.5. Suppose Alice plays optimally to avoid losing. She might choose x_1=0. Then at n=2, Bazza chooses x_2 = sqrt(2 - 0) = √2 ≈1.414. Then S_2=1.414, Q_2=2. At n=3, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 1.5. Since S_2=1.414, she can choose x_3 = 1.5 - 1.414 = 0.086, so S_3=1.5, Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + 0.0074 = 2.0074. Then at n=4, Bazza chooses x_4 = sqrt(4 - Q_3) = sqrt(4 - 2.0074) = sqrt(1.9926) ≈1.411. Then S_4 = S_3 + x_4 = 1.5 + 1.411 = 2.911, Q_4 = 4. Then at n=5, Alice must have S_5 ≤ 2.5. But S_4=2.911 > 2.5, so she cannot choose any x_5 ≥ 0 to satisfy S_5 ≤ 2.5 because S_5 = S_4 + x_5 ≥ 2.911 > 2.5. So she loses at n=5. So Bazza wins! Wait, but we need to check if Alice could have chosen a different x_1 to avoid this. She might choose x_1 not 0. Let's analyze the strategy: Bazza's strategy: always set Q_n = n at even n. That is, choose x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). He must ensure that Q_{n-1} ≤ n, which is true by induction if he plays this. Then we need to see if Alice can prevent S_{n-1} > λ n for all odd n. She can choose x_1 to be something else. Let's denote the state after n moves. At odd n, Alice chooses x_n. She wants to keep S_n as small as possible? Actually, she wants to avoid losing; she can choose x_n = 0 if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. But if she chooses x_n = 0, then S_n = S_{n-1}. Then Bazza at even n+1 will set x_{n+1} = sqrt((n+1) - Q_n). Q_n = Q_{n-1} + 0. So Q_n = Q_{n-1}. Then S_{n+1} = S_n + x_{n+1}. So the sum can increase. So she might try to choose x_n > 0 to keep the sum from getting too large? But she can also choose x_n = 0 to keep sum small. However, if she chooses x_n = 0, then Bazza's next move will increase the sum by sqrt((n+1) - Q_n). So the sum can become large. She might try to choose x_n positive to reduce the sum? No, adding positive increases sum. So she cannot reduce sum. She can only keep sum from increasing by choosing 0, but then Bazza will increase it. So she might try to choose x_n such that S_n is as small as possible? Actually, she wants to keep S_{n-1} ≤ λ n for all odd n. Since she controls x_n at odd n, she can choose x_n = 0 if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, which is the condition for her to move. So she will always choose 0 if possible. So her strategy is to always choose 0 at odd turns, as long as she can. Then the game reduces to: Alice always chooses 0, Bazza chooses x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) at even n. Then we need to see if this leads to a win for Bazza for some λ. Let's analyze this.

Let’s define the sequence with Alice always choosing 0. Then after each odd turn, S_n = S_{n-1} (since x_n=0). After each even turn, Bazza sets x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 = Q_{n-1} + (n - Q_{n-1}) = n. So Q_n = n for all even n. And S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i. Since x_1=0 (Alice chooses 0), then at n=2, x_2 = sqrt(2 - 0)=√2, so S_2=√2. At n=3, Alice chooses 0, so S_3=S_2=√2. At n=4, Bazza sets x_4 = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + 0 = 2. So x_4 = sqrt(4-2)=√2, S_4 = S_3 + √2 = √2+√2 = 2√2. At n=5, Alice chooses 0, so S_5 = 2√2. At n=6, Bazza sets x_6 = sqrt(6 - Q_5). Q_5 = Q_4 + x_5^2 = 4 + 0 = 4. So x_6 = sqrt(6-4)=√2, S_6 = S_5 + √2 = 2√2+√2=3√2. So by induction, at even n=2k, we have S_{2k} = k√2, Q_{2k}=2k. At odd n=2k+1, S_{2k+1}=k√2, Q_{2k+1}=2k. So the sum at even turns is k√2. Now, consider Alice's turn at odd n=2k+1. She needs S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). Since S_{2k}=k√2, the condition is k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1). For large k, this is approximately √2/2 λ? Actually, k√2 ≤ λ(2k+1) => √2 ≤ λ(2 + 1/k) => for large k, we need √2 ≤ 2λ => λ ≥ √2/2 ≈ 0.7071. So if λ < √2/2, then for sufficiently large k, k√2 > λ(2k+1), so Alice would lose at odd n=2k+1. But wait, she can choose x_{2k+1} = 0 only if S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). If not, she loses immediately. So if λ < √2/2, then eventually S_{2k} will exceed λ(2k+1). So Bazza wins by playing this strategy. But we need to check if Alice can deviate from always choosing 0 to avoid this. She might choose a positive x_n at some odd turn to reduce the sum? No, positive increases sum. So she cannot reduce sum. She could choose x_n = 0, which is the minimal. So if she chooses 0, the sum is as small as possible given the previous choices. But she might choose a positive x_n to make the sum larger? That would be worse. So her best chance to avoid losing is to keep sum as small as possible, i.e., always choose 0. So if λ < √2/2, then Bazza can force a win by always setting Q_n = n at even n, and Alice's best response is to choose 0, leading to eventual loss. But we need to verify that Alice cannot choose a different x_1 to avoid the eventual loss. For λ=0.5, √2/2 ≈0.7071, so λ=0.5 < 0.7071, so according to this, Bazza wins. But earlier we simulated with x_1=0 and found that at n=5, Alice loses because S_4=2√2≈2.828 > 2.5. So yes, Bazza wins. But could Alice choose a positive x_1 to make the sum smaller? No, positive x_1 increases S_2, making it worse. So she should choose x_1=0. So for λ=0.5, Bazza wins.

So for λ = √2/2, the condition holds with equality? Actually, k√2 ≤ √2 k + √2/2 is always true because the right side is larger by √2/2. So it holds. So for λ ≥ √2/2, the inequality holds for all k? So Alice can always choose 0 at odd turns, and the game continues forever. But we need to consider that Bazza might deviate from the strategy of setting Q_n = n at even n. He might choose a different x_n to try to force a win. So we need to analyze the game more generally.

Our analysis so far: For λ > √2, Alice wins immediately by choosing x_1 > √2 (since λ > √2, she can choose x_1 = √2+ε ≤ λ). For λ = √2, we need to see if Alice can force a win or Bazza can. For λ between √2/2 and √2, we need to see if Alice has a winning strategy. For λ < √2/2, we need to see if Bazza has a winning strategy.

But we also need to consider that Alice might not always choose 0; she might choose positive numbers to try to force a win. For λ large, she can win immediately. For λ small, Bazza might have a winning strategy. So the threshold might be λ = 1? Or λ = √2/2? Let's test λ = 0.8. Then λ > √2/2 ≈0.7071, so according to the asymptotic, if Alice always chooses 0, then S_{2k}=k√2, and condition k√2 ≤ 0.8(2k+1) => 1.414k ≤ 1.6k+0.8 => 0 ≤ 0.186k+0.8, always true. So she can avoid losing by always choosing 0. But does Bazza have a way to force a win? He could try to increase the sum of squares to the maximum, but then the sum also increases. However, if Alice always chooses 0, then Bazza's best response might be to set Q_n = n at even n, as we simulated, leading to eventual loss for him? Actually, for λ=0.8, we saw that S_{2k}=k√2, and the condition for Alice to move at odd n=2k+1 is S_{2k} ≤ 0.8(2k+1). Since k√2 ≤ 0.8(2k+1) is true for all k, she can always choose 0. So the game continues forever. But could Bazza adopt a different strategy to eventually make Alice lose? He might try to increase the sum S_n faster than the bound allows, by choosing large x_n at even turns. However, he is constrained by Q_n ≤ n. If he chooses a large x_n, then Q_n increases, but he must keep Q_n ≤ n. So he can set x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) to maximize the sum increase. That's exactly what he did. So his strategy is to maximize the sum increase at each even turn. This is a natural "greedy" strategy for Bazza to try to win. If this strategy does not cause Alice to lose, then perhaps Bazza cannot win. But we need to consider if Alice can deviate to avoid losing. For λ=0.8, if Alice always chooses 0, then the sum grows as k√2, and the condition holds, so she can continue forever. So the game does not end, and neither wins. So for λ=0.8, Alice can force a draw (by always choosing 0). But the problem asks for which λ Alice has a winning strategy, and which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. It does not mention draws; perhaps for some λ, the game is a draw if both play optimally? The problem statement: "Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." It does not ask for draws. So we need to find the λ such that one player can force a win (i.e., ensure that the opponent eventually cannot move). For λ=0.8, is there a winning strategy for Alice? She might try to force a win by choosing positive numbers to make Bazza unable to move. But she can also choose 0, leading to a draw. So if she has a winning strategy, she can force a win. But if she can only force a draw, then she does not have a winning strategy. So we need to see if she can force a win for λ=0.8. Let's try to see if she can adopt a strategy to win. For λ=0.8, she can choose x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2. He might choose x_2=0? But if he chooses x_2=0, then Q_2=0 ≤2, so he can. Then S_2=0. Then at n=3, Alice can choose x_3 up to 2.4. She could choose x_3=2.4, then S_3=2.4, Q_3=2.4^2=5.76. Then at n=4, Bazza needs Q_4 ≤4. But Q_3=5.76 >4, so he cannot move. So Alice wins! Wait, but she must ensure that after her move at n=3, the constraint S_3 ≤ λ*3 = 2.4 holds. If she chooses x_3=2.4, then S_3=2.4, which is ≤2.4, okay. So she wins immediately at n=4 because Bazza cannot move. But is this allowed? Let's check: At n=1, Alice chooses x_1=0. At n=2, Bazza chooses x_2=0. Then S_2=0, Q_2=0. At n=3, Alice chooses x_3=2.4. Then S_3=2.4, which is ≤ 0.8*3=2.4, okay. Q_3=0+0+5.76=5.76. At n=4, Bazza must choose x_4 ≥ 0 such that Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 ≤ 4. But Q_3=5.76 >4, so even with x_4=0, Q_4=5.76 >4, so he cannot satisfy the constraint. So he loses. So Alice wins. So for λ=0.8, Alice has a winning strategy: choose x_1=0, then after Bazza's move (which could be 0), choose x_3 = λ*3 - (x_1+x_2) = 2.4 - 0 = 2.4. But wait, what if Bazza chooses a positive x_2? He might try to avoid losing. If he chooses x_2 > 0, then S_2 = x_2, and Alice at n=3 can choose x_3 = 2.4 - x_2. Then Q_3 = x_2^2 + (2.4 - x_2)^2. For Bazza at n=4, he needs Q_4 ≤4. If Q_3 > 4, he loses. So we need to see if Bazza can choose x_2 to make Q_3 ≤4. Q_3 = x_2^2 + (2.4 - x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 4.8x_2 + 5.76. This is a quadratic with vertex at x_2 = 4.8/4 = 1.2. At x_2=1.2, Q_3 = 2*1.44 - 4.8*1.2 + 5.76 = 2.88 - 5.76 + 5.76 = 2.88. So the minimum is 2.88, which is <4. So Bazza can choose x_2=1.2, then Q_3=2.88, and then at n=4, he can choose x_4=0, then Q_4=2.88 ≤4, so he can move. So Alice's strategy of setting x_3=2.4 - x_2 might not win if Bazza chooses x_2 appropriately. So we need to see if Alice can adapt to force a win. Perhaps she can choose a different x_1? Let's analyze the game more systematically.

We need to find the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. The game is a kind of "alternating constraints" game where each player can choose numbers to maximize their chance. It might be related to the inequality between arithmetic mean and quadratic mean. Let's define the game in terms of "potential" functions.

Let’s denote the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The game ends when a player cannot move. For Alice at odd n, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, he loses if Q_{n-1} > n.

The players can choose any nonnegative x_n. They want to force the opponent into a position where the bound is exceeded.

We can think of the game as a "take-away" where each move adds a number, and the constraints are upper bounds on cumulative sums. The players can choose numbers to try to make the cumulative sum as large as possible or as small as possible. Since the game is infinite, we can consider strategies that aim to eventually exceed the bound.

Observation: For Alice, she can always choose x_n = 0 if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. So she can always avoid losing by choosing 0, provided that the sum before her turn is within the bound. So the only way she loses is if the sum before her turn exceeds λ n. So she wants to keep the sum small. But she also might want to win by making Bazza unable to move. She can do that by making the sum of squares large enough that at an even turn, the bound is exceeded. So she can try to increase Q_n to be > n at some even turn. But she can only increase Q_n by choosing a positive x_n at odd turns. So she can try to set x_n large to cause Q_n to exceed n at the next even turn. However, she must also respect her own constraint S_n ≤ λ n. So she cannot make x_n too large because that would increase S_n. There is a trade-off.

Similarly, Bazza can try to make S_n large enough that at an odd turn, S_n > λ (n+1). He can do that by choosing large x_n at even turns, but he must keep Q_n ≤ n.

Thus, the game is a kind of "alternating" where each player tries to push the cumulative sums to the limit.

We can try to find the "critical" λ where the game is a draw if both play optimally. Possibly the threshold is λ = 1? Let's test λ=1. We already found a winning strategy for Alice: choose x_1=0, then after Bazza's move, choose x_3 = 3 - x_2. But we need to check if Bazza can avoid losing. We simulated with Bazza always setting Q_n = n at even n, and found that Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3-x_2)^2, and the minimum is 4.5 > 4, so Bazza loses at n=4. But could Bazza choose a different x_2 to make Q_3 ≤4? The minimum of Q_3 is 4.5, so no. So Alice wins. But wait, we assumed that Alice chooses x_3 = 3 - x_2. Is that always possible? She must have x_3 ≥ 0, which is true because x_2 ≤ √2 < 3. So yes. So for λ=1, Alice wins. So λ=1 is in Alice's winning region.

Now test λ=0.9. Is there a winning strategy for Alice? Let's try to see if she can force a win. She can choose x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2. She wants to eventually make Q_n > n at some even turn. She can try to set x_3 = 0.9*3 - (x_1+x_2) = 2.7 - x_2. Then Q_3 = x_2^2 + (2.7 - x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 5.4x_2 + 7.29. The minimum occurs at x_2 = 5.4/4 = 1.35. The minimum value is 2*(1.8225) - 5.4*1.35 + 7.29 = 3.645 - 7.29 + 7.29 = 3.645. So Q_3 = 3.645. Then at n=4, Bazza needs Q_4 ≤4. He can choose x_4=0, then Q_4=3.645 ≤4, so he can move. Then at n=5, Alice can choose x_5 = 0.9*5 - S_4, where S_4 = x_1+x_2+x_3+x_4 = x_2 + (2.7 - x_2) = 2.7. So S_4=2.7. Then x_5 = 4.5 - 2.7 = 1.8. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + 1.8^2 = 3.645 + 3.24 = 6.885. At n=6, Bazza needs Q_6 ≤6. But Q_5=6.885 >6, so he cannot move. So Alice wins! But wait, we need to check if Bazza can choose a different x_2 to avoid this. He might choose x_2 such that Q_3 is even smaller? The minimum is 3.645, so no. But he could choose x_2 such that the sum S_4 is different? Actually, S_4 = x_2 + (2.7 - x_2) = 2.7 regardless of x_2, as long as Alice chooses x_3 = 2.7 - x_2. So S_4 is fixed at 2.7. Then at n=5, Alice chooses x_5 = 4.5 - S_4 = 1.8, which is positive. So Q_5 = Q_3 + 1.8^2. Since Q_3 is at least 3.645, Q_5 ≥ 3.645 + 3.24 = 6.885 >6. So Bazza loses at n=6. So Alice wins. So for λ=0.9, Alice wins.

Now test λ=0.8. We already saw that if Bazza chooses x_2=1.2, then Q_3=2.88, S_4=2.4, then at n=5, Alice can choose x_5 = 2.4 - S_4? Actually, λ*5=4.0? Wait, λ=0.8, so λ*5=4.0. Then S_676=471.1+1.4=472.5. Then at n=677, Alice chooses x_677 = 0.7*677 - S_676 = 474.9 - 472.5 = 2.4? Wait, 0.7*677 = 474.9? Actually, 677*0.7 = 474.9, yes. So x_677 = 474.9 - 472.5 = 2.4. That's not 1.4! Because S_676=472.5, and 0.7*677=474.9, so x_677 = 2.4. So the pattern changes. But we need to see if Bazza can still move at n=678. At n=677, Alice chooses x_677 = 2.4. Then Q_677 = Q_676 + 2.4^2. Q_676 = 660.765? Actually, we had Q_675 = 660.765, and at n=676, Bazza chooses x_676 = sqrt(676 - Q_675) = sqrt(676 - 660.765) = sqrt(15.235) ≈3.904. So Q_676 = 676. So Q_676 = 676. Then at n=677, Alice chooses x_677 = 2.4, so Q_677 = 676 + 5.76 = 681.76. At n=678, Bazza needs Q_678 ≤678. But Q_677 = 681.76 >678, so he cannot move. So Alice wins at n=678. So for λ=0.7, Alice wins by playing this strategy? But earlier we simulated with Alice always choosing 0 and Bazza always setting Q_n=n, and we found that at n=103, Alice loses because S_102 > 0.7*103? Actually, we need to check the asymptotic condition: For λ=0.7, the critical condition from the "greedy" strategy for Bazza is that if Alice always chooses 0, then S_{2k}=k√2, and the condition for Alice to move at odd n=2k+1 is S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) => k√2 ≤ 0.7(2k+1) => √2 ≤ 1.4 + 0.7/k => for large k, √2 ≈1.414 >1.4, so eventually the inequality fails. That means that if Alice always chooses 0, then eventually Bazza will win because Alice cannot move. But in our strategy, Alice does not always choose 0; she chooses x_3 = 2.1 - x_2, which is positive, and then later she chooses x_5 = 3.5 - S_4, etc. This changes the sum. So the "greedy" strategy for Bazza (always set Q_n = n) is not necessarily optimal if Alice plays adaptively. We need to analyze the game properly.

We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. The game is sequential with perfect information. We can try to find a "strategy stealing" or "potential function" that gives a threshold.

Let's think of the game in terms of "critical sequences". Define the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The game ends when a player cannot move. For Alice at odd n, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, he loses if Q_{n-1} > n.

We can try to find a "winning condition" for Alice. She wants to force Bazza to be unable to move at some even turn. That means she wants to make Q_n > n for some even n. She can increase Q_n by choosing a positive x_n at odd turns. However, she must also keep S_n ≤ λ n. So she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. So the maximum increase in Q_n she can achieve at odd n is (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can try to push Q_n above n.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some odd turn. He can increase S_n by choosing positive x_n at even turns, but he must keep Q_n ≤ n. So the maximum increase he can achieve at even n is sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So he can try to push S_n above λ (n+1).

Thus, the game is like a "race" where each player tries to push the cumulative sums to the limit. The players can choose numbers to maximize their own chance. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit-evasion" game.

We can try to find the "critical" λ such that the game is a draw if both play optimally. For λ > something, Alice can force a win; for λ < something, Bazza can force a win; for λ = something, maybe draw.

Let's try to analyze the game using "potential" functions. Define the "gap" for Alice: She wants to make Q_n - n > 0 at some even n. The maximum she can increase Q_n at odd n is (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can try to make Q_n large enough.

But we need to consider that Bazza can also increase S_n to make Alice unable to move. So we need to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that she can always make a move and eventually force Bazza to be unable to move.

Observation: If λ ≥ 1, then the condition S_n ≤ λ n is automatically satisfied if Q_n ≤ n? Not exactly, but we can try to see if Alice can win for λ ≥ 1. We already have λ > √2 gives immediate win. For λ = 1, we found a winning strategy: choose x_1=0, then after Bazza's move, choose x_3 = 3 - x_2. But we need to verify that this strategy works for all possible Bazza responses. Let's analyze the game for λ=1 systematically.

Game for λ=1: Alice moves on odd n, Bazza on even n. Alice's constraint: sum ≤ n. Bazza's constraint: sum of squares ≤ n.

At n=1, Alice chooses x_1 ∈ [0,1]. She wants to win. She can choose x_1=0. Then at n=2, Bazza chooses x_2 ≥ 0 such that x_1^2+x_2^2 ≤ 2, so x_2^2 ≤ 2, x_2 ≤ √2. He can choose any x_2 in [0,√2]. He wants to avoid losing. At n=3, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 = x_1+x_2+x_3 ≤ 3. Since x_1=0, she can choose x_3 = 3 - x_2 (which is ≥ 3-√2 ≈1.586). Then Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3-x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 6x_2 + 9. This is a quadratic with minimum at x_2=1.5, value 2*2.25 - 9 + 9 = 4.5. So Q_3 ≥ 4.5. At n=4, Bazza must choose x_4 such that Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 ≤ 4. Since Q_3 ≥ 4.5 >4, even with x_4=0, Q_4 ≥ 4.5 >4, so he cannot satisfy the constraint. Thus, he loses at n=4. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, Alice has a winning strategy (choose x_1=0, then x_3=3-x_2). So λ=1 is in Alice's winning region.

Now, what about λ=0.9? We already found a winning strategy: choose x_1=0, then x_3=2.7 - x_2, then at n=5, Alice chooses x_5=4.5 - S_4, etc. But we need to check if Bazza can deviate to avoid losing. We need to analyze the game for λ=0.9 more generally.

Let's try to see if for λ=0.9, Alice can force a win by a similar strategy: She chooses x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2. She then chooses x_3 = 0.9*3 - x_2 = 2.7 - x_2. Then S_3 = x_2 + (2.7 - x_2) = 2.7. Q_3 = x_2^2 + (2.7 - x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 5.4x_2 + 7.29. The minimum of this quadratic is at x_2=1.35, value 2*(1.8225) - 5.4*1.35 + 7.29 = 3.645 - 7.29 + 7.29 = 3.645. So Q_3 ≥ 3.645. At n=4, Bazza chooses x_4 such that Q_4 ≤ 4. He can choose x_4 = sqrt(4 - Q_3). Since Q_3 ≥ 3.645, the maximum possible x_4 is sqrt(4 - 3.645) = sqrt(0.355) ≈0.596. He could also choose x_4=0. So Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 ≤ 4. He will likely choose x_4 to maximize his chance to win later. He might choose x_4=0 to keep sum small. Then S_4 = S_3 + x_4 = 2.7. Q_4 = Q_3. Then at n=5, Alice must choose x_5 such that S_5 ≤ 4.5. She can choose x_5 = 4.5 - S_4 = 4.5 - 2.7 = 1.8. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + 1.8^2 = Q_3 + 3.24. Since Q_3 ≥ 3.645, Q_5 ≥ 6.885. At n=6, Bazza must have Q_6 ≤ 6. He can choose x_6 = sqrt(6 - Q_5). Since Q_5 ≥ 6.885 >6, even with x_6=0, Q_6 = Q_5 >6, so he cannot move. Thus, he loses at n=6. So Alice wins. So for λ=0.9, the strategy works regardless of Bazza's choice of x_2? We assumed Bazza chooses x_4=0. But what if he chooses x_4 positive? Then S_4 increases, and at n=5, Alice's x_5 = 4.5 - S_4 might be smaller. However, Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 ≤ 4, so Q_4 is at most 4. Then Q_5 = Q_4 + x_5^2 ≤ 4 + (4.5 - S_4)^2. Since S_4 = 2.7 + x_4, we have x_5 = 4.5 - (2.7 + x_4) = 1.8 - x_4. So Q_5 ≤ 4 + (1.8 - x_4)^2. Also, Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2, and Q_3 ≥ 3.645. So Q_5 ≥ Q_3 + x_4^2 + (1.8 - x_4)^2 = 3.645 + x_4^2 + (1.8 - x_4)^2. Let's compute the minimum of this expression over x_4 ∈ [0, sqrt(4 - Q_3)]? Actually, x_4 can be any number such that Q_4 ≤ 4, i.e., Q_3 + x_4^2 ≤ 4. Since Q_3 ≥ 3.645, the maximum x_4 is sqrt(4 - Q_3) ≤ sqrt(0.355) ≈0.596. So x_4 is in [0, 0.596]. Then f(x_4) = x_4^2 + (1.8 - x_4)^2 = 2x_4^2 - 3.6x_4 + 3.24. This is a quadratic with vertex at x_4 = 3.6/4 = 0.9. Since x_4 ≤0.596, the function is decreasing on [0,0.596]? Actually, the derivative is 4x_4 - 3.6, which is negative for x_4 <0.9. So f is decreasing on [0,0.596]. The minimum on the interval is at the right endpoint x_4=0.596, giving f(0.596) = 2*(0.355) - 3.6*0.596 + 3.24 = 0.71 - 2.1456 + 3.24 = 1.8044. So Q_5 ≥ 3.645 + 1.8044 = 5.4494. Then Q_5 ≤ 4 + (1.8 - 0.596)^2 = 4 + (1.204)^2 = 4 + 1.4496 = 5.4496. So Q_5 is exactly 5.4494? Actually, the lower bound is 5.4494, the upper bound is 5.4496, so Q_5 is around 5.4495. That is less than 6. So at n=6, Bazza can choose x_6 = sqrt(6 - Q_5) ≈ sqrt(0.5505) ≈0.742. Then Q_6 = 6. Then S_6 = S_5 + x_6. S_5 = S_4 + x_5 = (2.7 + x_4) + (1.8 - x_4) = 4.5. So S_5 = 4.5. Then S_6 = 4.5 + 0.742 = 5.242. Then at n=7, Alice must choose x_7 such that S_7 ≤ 6.3. She can choose x_7 = 6.3 - 5.242 = 1.058. Then Q_7 = Q_6 + 1.058^2 = 6 + 1.119 = 7.119. Then at n=8, Bazza needs Q_8 ≤8. He can choose x_8 = sqrt(8 - 7.119) = sqrt(0.881) ≈0.939. Then Q_8 = 8. S_8 = S_7 + x_8 = (5.242+1.058) + 0.939 = 6.3+0.939=7.239? Wait, S_7 = 5.242+1.058=6.3. So S_8 = 6.3+0.939=7.239. Then at n=9, Alice needs S_9 ≤ 6.9? Actually, λ*9 = 8.1? No, λ=0.9, so λ*9 = 8.1. S_8=7.239, so she can choose x_9 = 8.1 - 7.239 = 0.861. Then Q_9 = 8 + 0.861^2 = 8 + 0.741 = 8.741. Then at n=10, Bazza needs Q_10 ≤10. He can choose x_10 = sqrt(10 - 8.741) = sqrt(1.259) ≈1.122. Then Q_10 = 10. Thus, at n=4, Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, Alice has a winning strategy: choose x_1=0, then after Bazza's move, choose x_3 = 3 - x_2. So we need to analyze the game more systematically.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly the answer is: Alice wins for λ ≥ 1, Bazza wins for λ < 1? But S_{n-1} is the sum of x_i for i=1..n-1. Since Bazza controls even moves, he can try to increase the sum by choosing large x_n at even turns. However, he must keep Q_n ≤ n. So if he chooses large x_n, that increases Q_n, and he might be forced to keep Q_n ≤ n, so he cannot make x_n too large. There is a trade-off.

We can think of the game as a kind of "alternating constraints" where each player can choose numbers to maximize their own chance of winning. Perhaps we can find a "strategy stealing" or "potential function" that gives a threshold.

Let's define the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The game ends when a player cannot move. For Alice at odd n, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, he loses if Q_{n-1} > n.

Thus, the game is similar to a "take-away" where each player adds a nonnegative number, and the constraints are upper bounds on cumulative sums. The players can choose any number within the allowed range. They want to force the opponent into a position where the bound is exceeded.

We can try to analyze the game by considering the "critical" sequences. Perhaps we can find a strategy for Alice to win if λ ≥ 1, and for Bazza to win if λ < 1. But we need to verify for λ=1 we already found a winning strategy for Alice. For λ=0.5, we need to see if Bazza can force a win. Let's try to see if Bazza can adopt a strategy that eventually makes S_{n-1} > λ n for some odd n. Since λ=0.5, λ n = n/2. So he wants to make the sum before Alice's turn exceed n/2. The sum is cumulative; at odd n, the sum before her turn is S_{n-1}. He can try to increase the sum by choosing large x at even turns. But he must also keep Q_n ≤ n. So if he chooses x_n large, that increases Q_n, and he might need to keep Q_n ≤ n. So there is a constraint linking the sum and sum of squares.

We can try to see if Bazza can force a win by playing a "greedy" strategy: always choose x_n as large as possible, i.e., such that Q_n = n (the maximum allowed). Then at even n, he sets x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Then the sum S_n increases by that amount. Then Alice at odd n+1 must have S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n is already > λ (n+1), she loses. So Bazza wants to make S_n large enough that at the next odd turn, S_n > λ (n+1). Since he controls even moves, he can try to push S_n up.

Let's simulate for λ=0.5. Suppose Alice plays optimally to avoid losing. She might choose x_1=0. Then at n=2, Bazza chooses x_2 = sqrt(2 - 0) = √2 ≈1.414. Then S_2=1.414, Q_2=2. At n=3, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 1.5. Since S_2=1.414, she can choose x_3 = 1.5 - 1.414 = 0.086, so S_3=1.5, Q_3 = Q_2 + x_3^2 = 2 + 0.0074 = 2.0074. Let's analyze the game more systematically.

We need to find the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. The game is a kind of "alternating constraints" game where each player can choose numbers to maximize their chance. It might be related to the inequality between arithmetic mean and quadratic mean. Let's define the game in terms of "potential" functions.

Let’s denote the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The game ends when a player cannot move. For Alice at odd n, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, he loses if Q_{n-1} > n.

The players can choose any nonnegative x_n. They want to force the opponent into a position where the bound is exceeded.

We can think of the game as a "take-away" where each move adds a number, and the constraints are upper bounds on cumulative sums. The players can choose numbers to try to make the cumulative sum as large as possible or as small as possible. Since the game is infinite, we can consider strategies that aim to eventually exceed the bound.

Observation: For Alice, she can always choose x_n = 0 if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. So she can always avoid losing by choosing 0, provided that the sum before her turn is within the bound. So the only way she loses is if the sum before her turn exceeds λ n. So she wants to keep the sum small. But she also might want to win by making Bazza unable to move. She can do that by making the sum of squares large enough that at an even turn, the bound is exceeded. So she can try to increase Q_n to be > n at some even turn. But she can only increase Q_n by choosing a positive x_n at odd turns. So she can try to set x_n large to cause Q_n to exceed n at the next even turn. However, she must also respect her own constraint S_n ≤ λ n. So she cannot make x_n too large because that would increase S_n. There is a trade-off.

Similarly, Bazza can try to make S_n large enough that at an odd turn, S_n > λ (n+1). He can do that by choosing large x_n at even turns, but he must keep Q_n ≤ n.

Thus, the game is a kind of "alternating" where each player tries to push the cumulative sums to the limit.

We can try to find the "critical" λ where the game is a draw if both play optimally. Possibly the threshold is λ = 1? Let's test λ=1. We already found a winning strategy for Alice: choose x_1=0, then after Bazza's move, choose x_3 = 3 - x_2. But we need to check if Bazza can avoid losing. We simulated with Bazza always setting Q_n = n at even n, and found that Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3-x_2)^2, and the minimum is 4.5 > 4, so Bazza loses at n=4. But could Bazza choose a different x_2 to make Q_3 ≤4? The minimum of Q_3 is 4.5, so no. So Alice wins. But wait, we assumed that Alice chooses x_3 = 3 - x_2. Is that always possible? She must have x_3 ≥ 0, which is true because x_2 ≤ √2 < 3. So yes. So for λ=1, Alice wins. So λ=1 is in Alice's winning region.

Now test λ=0.9. Is there a winning strategy for Alice? Let's try to see if she can force a win. She can choose x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2. She wants to eventually make Q_n > n at some even turn. The minimum value is 2*(1.8225) - 5.4*1.35 + 7.29 = 3.645 - 7.29 + 7.29 = 3.645. So Q_3 = 3.645. Then at n=4, Bazza needs Q_4 ≤4. He can choose x_4=0, then Q_4=3.645 ≤4, so he can move. So for λ=0.7, Alice wins by playing this strategy? But earlier we simulated with Alice always choosing 0 and Bazza always setting Q_n=n, and we found that at n=103, Alice loses because S_102 > 0.7*103? Actually, we need to check the asymptotic condition: For λ=0.7, the critical condition from the "greedy" strategy for Bazza is that if Alice always chooses 0, then S_{2k}=k√2, and the condition for Alice to move at odd n=2k+1 is S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) => k√2 ≤ 0.7(2k+1) => √2 ≤ 1.4 + 0.7/k => for large k, √2 ≈1.414 >1.4, so eventually the inequality fails. That means that if Alice always chooses 0, then eventually Bazza will win because Alice cannot move. But in our strategy, Alice does not always choose 0; she chooses x_3 = 2.1 - x_2, which is positive, and then later she chooses x_5 = 3.5 - S_4, etc. This changes the sum. So the "greedy" strategy for Bazza (always set Q_n = n) is not necessarily optimal if Alice plays adaptively. We need to analyze the game properly.

We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. The game is sequential with perfect information. We can try to find a "strategy stealing" or "potential function" that gives a threshold.

Let's think of the game in terms of "critical sequences". Define the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The game ends when a player cannot move. For Alice at odd n, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n. For Bazza at even n, he loses if Q_{n-1} > n.

We can try to find a "winning condition" for Alice. She wants to force Bazza to be unable to move at some even turn. That means she wants to make Q_n > n for some even n. She can increase Q_n by choosing a positive x_n at odd turns. However, she must also keep S_n ≤ λ n. So she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. So the maximum increase in Q_n she can achieve at odd n is (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can try to push Q_n above n.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1) at some odd turn. He can increase S_n by choosing positive x_n at even turns, but he must keep Q_n ≤ n. So the maximum increase he can achieve at even n is sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So he can try to push S_n above λ (n+1).

Thus, the game is like a "race" where each player tries to push the cumulative sums to the limit. The players can choose numbers to maximize their own chance. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit-evasion" game.

We can try to find the "critical" λ such that the game is a draw if both play optimally. For λ > something, Alice can force a win; for λ < something, Bazza can force a win; for λ = something, maybe draw.

Let's try to analyze the game using "potential" functions. Define the "gap" for Alice: She wants to make Q_n - n > 0 at some even n. The maximum she can increase Q_n at odd n is (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can try to make Q_n large enough.

But we need to consider that Bazza can also increase S_n to make Alice unable to move. So we need to find a strategy for Alice that ensures that she can always make a move and eventually force Bazza to be unable to move.

Observation: If λ ≥ 1, then the condition S_n ≤ λ n is automatically satisfied if Q_n ≤ n? Not exactly, but we can try to see if Alice can win for λ ≥ 1. We already have λ > √2 gives immediate win. For λ = 1, we found a winning strategy: choose x_1=0, then after Bazza's move, choose x_3 = 3 - x_2. But we need to verify that this strategy works for all possible Bazza responses. Let's analyze the game for λ=1 systematically.

Game for λ=1: Alice moves on odd n, Bazza on even n. Alice's constraint: sum ≤ n. Bazza's constraint: sum of squares ≤ n.

At n=1, Alice chooses x_1 ∈ [0,1]. She wants to win. She can choose x_1=0. Then at n=2, Bazza chooses x_2 ≥ 0 such that x_1^2+x_2^2 ≤ 2, so x_2^2 ≤ 2, x_2 ≤ √2. He can choose any x_2 in [0,√2]. He wants to avoid losing. At n=3, Alice must choose x_3 such that S_3 = x_1+x_2+x_3 ≤ 3. Since x_1=0, she can choose x_3 = 3 - x_2 (which is ≥ 3-√2 ≈1.586). Then Q_3 = x_2^2 + (3-x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 6x_2 + 9. This is a quadratic with minimum at x_2=1.5, value 2*2.25 - 9 + 9 = 4.5. So Q_3 ≥ 4.5. At n=4, Bazza must choose x_4 such that Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 ≤ 4. Since Q_3 ≥ 4.5 >4, even with x_4=0, Q_4 ≥ 4.5 >4, so he cannot satisfy the constraint. Thus, he loses at n=4. So Alice wins. So for λ=1, Alice has a winning strategy (choose x_1=0, then x_3=3-x_2). So λ=1 is in Alice's winning region.

Now, what about λ=0.9? We already found a winning strategy: choose x_1=0, then x_3=2.7 - x_2, then at n=5, Alice chooses x_5=4.5 - S_4, etc. But we need to check if Bazza can deviate to avoid losing. We need to analyze the game for λ=0.9 more generally.

Let's try to see if for λ=0.9, Alice can force a win by a similar strategy: She chooses x_1=0. Then Bazza chooses x_2. She then chooses x_3 = 0.9*3 - x_2 = 2.7 - x_2. Then S_3 = x_2 + (2.7 - x_2) = 2.7. Q_3 = x_2^2 + (2.7 - x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 5.4x_2 + 7.29.
